<div id="review_1066256839" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1066256839" />
    <a title="Seemita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita"><img alt="Seemita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473185997p2/34622220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1066256839?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seemita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seemita" href="/user/show/34622220-seemita">Seemita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=other-awards-w">other-awards-w</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=booker-sl-ll">booker-sl-ll</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34622220-seemita?shelf=for_legacy">for_legacy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1066256839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16826483299763177176"><i>
  <b>"In everyone's life, there are people who stay and people who go and people who are taken against their will."</b>
</i><br/><br/>Who do you remember the most? I asked myself. Those who make part of my primary circle of existence and have enriched my being with their presence? Or those who came and then departed for good, leaving an indelible mark on my life, as it looks today? Or those who, by all means that I could fathom, were supposed to be a part of my life but were disengaged from me with a menacing strik</span>
  <span id="freeText16826483299763177176" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>"In everyone's life, there are people who stay and people who go and people who are taken against their will."</b>
</i><br /><br />Who do you remember the most? I asked myself. Those who make part of my primary circle of existence and have enriched my being with their presence? Or those who came and then departed for good, leaving an indelible mark on my life, as it looks today? Or those who, by all means that I could fathom, were supposed to be a part of my life but were disengaged from me with a menacing strike of destiny? I can’t say, honestly. Then I rearranged the words and popped the question to myself again. This time, the answer was clear. <br /><br />The question: <i>Who do I miss the most?</i><br /><br />Rosemary Cooke is in a swamp of agony and pain. She gets up one morning and realizes that her sister, Fern, has been taken away from her. After feigning audacity for many summers and winters, she, at last, acknowledges her inability to overcome this emotional ordeal. She does not <i>
  <b>want</b>
</i> to overcome, in fact. She tries to piece together the <i>being</i> from the vivid <i>memories</i> she has. The faint curtains of childhood plays and innocent tricks that swooshes at her fiery heart cannot veil her helplessness that burns her day and night. She knocks relentlessly at the doors of her parents but hears nothing more than the excruciating silence of their soldered hearts. She pounds, with a maddening howl, the trails of her wayward brother, in search of one, tiny clue to unite her with Fern. But her brother reflects her emptiness like a mirror and she stumbles upon her discolored self in his disturbing, almost resigned, eyes. Amid random strangers and nagging neighbors, erudite academicians and gullible students, she spins her mind, sending darts of questions in all directions, expecting at least one of them to echo her torment and send some optimistic air back at her. But the spinning does not yield anything more than a nauseating headache, reminiscent of her ever-sinking heart.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>"An off-told story is like a photograph in a family album. Eventually it replaces the moment it was meant to capture."</b>
</i><br /><br />Does Fern reduce to a picture in her family album? Or she springs to life every time a loved one’s warm breath passes over her static smile? Does Rosemary give up on her only sister? In the blinding world of incorrigible beings, treacherous acts and calamitous events, can Fern’s whereabouts be a shining light on a tower house, visible from a distance and untouched by the swarming sea of awful things beneath? Is she even alive?<br /><br />I thought it worthwhile to walk along with Rosemary and see where her hunt leads her to. And I must say the journey turned out to be a momentous one.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I came to know that Fern was a chimpanzee; a twin who bears a five-year long experiment of Rosemary's Father, a Psychologist. When the experiment gets aborted, Fern gets abandoned to live a life with species of <i>her</i> own kind. But how far should the fragility of human existence that floats over the experimental waters of scientific quests be pursued?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Fowler glorifies the bond of emotion that overgrows convention when tended with sensitivity, sacrifice, courage and love; when one does not need an endorsement from family and society and the warmth of a relationship is sufficient to melt the hardest of obstacles into nothingness; when we can love someone with such madness that the love does not die ever and hangs like a fragrant air for evenings together, long after we have taken divergent paths. <br /><br />I stood looking at Rosemary when she reached the last point of her voyage. I shed a tear. Something took a roll inside me. I placed a hand on my heart and caressed it. We all love atleast someone in our lives like that. And we know, <i>that</i> love will never be over.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16826483299763177176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phrynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phrynne" href="/user/show/8655084-phrynne">Phrynne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer944747886" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14055892023595468292">Just two stars for this one because for me it was just an okay book. No spoilers but the big reveal about Fern which is supposed to make the book more interesting just ruined it for me. I was expecting one thing and got another and it all became a bit too wacko for my taste. Rosemary was a very weak, unlikeable character and I just wanted to smack Harlow. And then for a big finale we discover that Rosemary's memory of events was not to be relied upon anyway. So basically it was all for nothing.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_632005402" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/632005402" />
    <a title="~✡~Dαni(ela) ♥ ♂♂ love &amp; semi-colons~✡~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7999417-d-ni-ela-love-semi-colons"><img alt="~✡~Dαni(ela) ♥ ♂♂ love &amp; semi-colons~✡~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485131784p2/7999417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/632005402?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="~✡~Dαni(ela) ♥ ♂♂ love &amp; semi-colons~✡~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="~✡~Dαni(ela) ♥ ♂♂ love &amp; semi-colons~✡~" href="/user/show/7999417-d-ni-ela-love-semi-colons">~✡~Dαni(ela) ♥ ♂♂ love &amp; semi-colons~✡~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer632005402" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16886064628123189753"><i>Due to various comments regarding the "spoilers" in my original review, I have added spoiler tags below. Disclaimer: I do NOT think this review contains spoilers. I'm super careful of this and would never give away an essential surprise of a story. The so-called "spoilers" are the main plot point and are described in detail in the description of the book found above. So if you've read the book description or read anything about this novel in a magazine review or author interview, don't worry abo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16886064628123189753" style="display:none"><i>Due to various comments regarding the "spoilers" in my original review, I have added spoiler tags below. Disclaimer: I do NOT think this review contains spoilers. I'm super careful of this and would never give away an essential surprise of a story. The so-called "spoilers" are the main plot point and are described in detail in the description of the book found above. So if you've read the book description or read anything about this novel in a magazine review or author interview, don't worry about the spoiler tags! But if you're the kind who wants to know nothing about a book before you start reading, go ahead and worry - and don't read the comments! </i> <br /><br />Fern, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a chimpanzee, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and Rosemary are raised together as siblings; they are practically twins. In 1979, when Rosie is 5, Fern disappears. Six years later, Rosie's older brother, Lowell, follows suit. It takes Rosie until she's in her 20s to figure out why (on both counts).<br /><br />This books starts in the middle, in the mid-90s, when Rosie is in college. She's been in college for a while and has no idea what to do with herself. She likes being a student. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She suppresses the "otherness" in herself, the chimpanzee part, which her elementary schoolmates dubbed "monkey girl."  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> No one at UC Davis knows anything about her, and she wants to keep it that way. Enter Harlow, a wild, wanton drama major, who pulls Rosie out of her shell (and gets her arrested to boot). Harlow and her boyfriend Reg become part of Rosie's story, as do Todd, her roommate, and Ezra, the intense CIA wannabe who is the manager of Rosie's apartment building.<br /><br />Fowler's novel isn't linear. We get glimpses of memories, usually analyzed with a caveat that memories are often inaccurate (Rosie's dad is a psychologist, so she knows a lot about this, and tells us so). <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Rosie doesn't tell us that Fern is a chimp until we're 20 percent into the book (to be fair, the book description reveals this, so it's not much of a giveaway). <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> We don't find out what happened to Lowell until we're more than half-way through. And we don't meet Fern until the end, and even then, there's more, because what story really ends? <br /><br />I was frustrated with this book at first; some of the college scenes felt drawn out. But Rosie's voice rang true, as did her relationship with Fern. I am not an animal-rights activist, but while working on my master's in linguistics I studied chimpanzee language acquisition, and I am certainly anti animal experimentation and for the humane treatment of animals. If you're not, this book will change your mind. This is as much Fern's story, as Rosie's story. It's the story of the non-human animal who, in many ways, is more human than we are. It's about losing a sibling, then losing yourself. It's about yearning for something true. And finding it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16886064628123189753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_866567378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/866567378" />
    <a title="Scott Rhee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee"><img alt="Scott Rhee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408223108p2/5717792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/866567378?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Rhee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Rhee" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee">Scott Rhee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=dysfunctional-family-drama">dysfunctional-family-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=animal-rights">animal-rights</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer866567378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9577792076492743215">Someone recommended Karen Joy Fowler's novel "We Are All Completely Beside Ourselves", and, I'll be honest, I was hesitant to read it because she also wrote "The Jane Austen Book Club", a book which I haven't read but which seemed like the kind of chick lit that I can't stand---a group of women from all walks of life, bonding over some kind of club (be it knitting, cooking, books, traveling pants, or Downton Abbey), talking about how shitty their relationships are or reminiscing about the dream </span>
  <span id="freeText9577792076492743215" style="display:none">Someone recommended Karen Joy Fowler's novel "We Are All Completely Beside Ourselves", and, I'll be honest, I was hesitant to read it because she also wrote "The Jane Austen Book Club", a book which I haven't read but which seemed like the kind of chick lit that I can't stand---a group of women from all walks of life, bonding over some kind of club (be it knitting, cooking, books, traveling pants, or Downton Abbey), talking about how shitty their relationships are or reminiscing about the dream guy who got away in college or the one time they visited Paris yadda yadda yadda---which I realize is the worst kind of book snobbery and blatant prejudice but, hey, I'm just being honest. Anyway, someone recommended this book, her latest, and I'm glad they did. <br /><br />"WAACBO" is a phenomenal, brilliant, beautiful, amazing, thought-provoking tear-jerker of a novel, and I can't say too much about it without dropping spoilers, but it's basically, bottom line, a book that questions the definition of the word "human". <br /><br />It's the story of a young woman named Rosemary Cooke, who is a socially awkward college student with a dysfunctional family history. Her older brother is on the lam from the FBI, and she has some troubling issues with her "memories" of growing up with her parents, her brother, and sister. She also had an imaginary friend named Mary. Then there are the numerous grad students that came and went throughout her childhood. Her childhood was a pretty normal childhood, except for one important fact: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ her sister, Fern, was a chimpanzee.<br /><br />Something happened when she was younger that resulted in Fern being sent away, and the family immediately fell apart afterwards. Unfortunately, Rosemary can't (or won't) remember what happened, exactly. It was something bad; so bad that she has created "alternative" memories to mask them. It was so bad that her brother disappeared to lead a life of crime, but a crime that's arguably an honorable one: he became an animal rights activist, an activity that the government considers "domestic terrorism".<br /><br />When her brother shows up at her college dorm one day, out of the blue, after nearly a decade of silence, her memories begin to return.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Fowler's novel is that rare novel that touches on important, even controversial, themes without being preachy or pedagogic. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Her implied message that animals have souls---a belief that goes against the humanocentrism of Judeo-Christian theology---will, for some, like me, be preaching to the choir. It will probably raise the ire and annoyance of others. So be it.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This is my pick for Best Novel of 2013. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9577792076492743215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_951760449" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/951760449" />
    <a title="Leanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5873788-leanne"><img alt="Leanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560475582p2/5873788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/951760449?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leanne" href="/user/show/5873788-leanne">Leanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5873788-leanne?shelf=my-heart-hurts">my-heart-hurts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5873788-leanne?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer951760449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10780243334018174085">This isn't going to be a long review, because I don't want to ruin this book for anyone. Seriously, don't click on the title. Don't read any other reviews. Because all I knew going into <i>We Are All Completely Beside Ourselves</i> was that it was about some type of dysfunctional family. And really, that can mean anything. And it's <i>so much better</i> that way!<br/><br/>I loved the tone of the book, the dry humour and the elegance of the writing, how it's entertaining but appropriately sad and serious at times. The n</span>
  <span id="freeText10780243334018174085" style="display:none">This isn't going to be a long review, because I don't want to ruin this book for anyone. Seriously, don't click on the title. Don't read any other reviews. Because all I knew going into <i>We Are All Completely Beside Ourselves</i> was that it was about some type of dysfunctional family. And really, that can mean anything. And it's <i>so much better</i> that way!<br /><br />I loved the tone of the book, the dry humour and the elegance of the writing, how it's entertaining but appropriately sad and serious at times. The narrator, Rosemary Cooke, isn't altogether likeable - she's a little bit odd, and rightfully so - and the structure is a little bit all over the place, but that was something I liked. Our quirky main character tells a quirky story with quirky form and it all comes together beautifully. (I don't mean quirky in a Wes Anderson kind of way, because it's not like that at all, but it's just a little bit different - in a good way!) It's an accurate depiction of childhood, and the foggy half memories that come back when you reflect on that childhood. It explores interesting scientific, psychological, and ethical issues without being preachy or overly technical. It makes you think, and it makes your heart hurt a little bit. It's really quite good - I think it's one of those stories that I've rated 4 stars and will come to appreciate more over time, rather than something that fades from memory and after several months should really be knocked down to a 3.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10780243334018174085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1304034776?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1304034776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2477503748036862539">2.5* rounded up.<br/><br/>I live under a rock. I do. Otherwise, I would have probably known what the big twist in this book was all about. But I didn't, and that was ok.<br/> <br/>However, even not knowing that there even is a twist didn't help me enjoy the book more than I did, and here is why:<br/> <br/>The beginning of the story was awesome - the MC, Rosemary Cooke, is inadvertently arrested when another woman goes mental on her ex-boyfriend in a cafeteria.<br/>What a great start to a story! <br/> <br/>Unfortunately, from there on the b</span>
  <span id="freeText2477503748036862539" style="display:none">2.5* rounded up.<br /><br />I live under a rock. I do. Otherwise, I would have probably known what the big twist in this book was all about. But I didn't, and that was ok.<br /> <br />However, even not knowing that there even is a twist didn't help me enjoy the book more than I did, and here is why:<br /> <br />The beginning of the story was awesome - the MC, Rosemary Cooke, is inadvertently arrested when another woman goes mental on her ex-boyfriend in a cafeteria.<br />What a great start to a story! <br /> <br />Unfortunately, from there on the book lost momentum while Rosemary tells about her childhood and introduces us to her family, and how her and her family's lives have fallen apart after her sister and brother disappeared. There is also the "big reveal", after which the book continues in search for a conclusion.<br /> <br />Fowler takes up an interesting story full of thoughtful observations and quirky dialogue, but I could not help feeling that the issues Fowler tried to address where over-written and drawn out to an extent where I lost interest. What's more, even my usual enthusiasm for animal welfare issues and philosophy near enough extinguished over the question what the book was trying to do. <br />Did the interjections of literary and philosophical quotations try to aid the message of the book or were they meant to lend an air of validation to what would have otherwise been a perfectly good coming of age story or family saga?<br /> <br />I know this book has received a lot of praise but it just did not do much for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2477503748036862539" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('flCwwLBunqmS5vgWlsSwDaauMaqi9UAooDViOtTa00dpSazjIUAXilAp5qL8hJCFVI+utvPCeya+Bpn/PTZ4aw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('T1KU4aRExgqlDjK3QAXG/Z2ln2nt5xNl1CiF0VmsPTVYS4jCNWpPKWfBLAMqReZ1b4QAdbzQKGvKG34UsECWGQ==')})
  }

Lovers at school, the two young women now perform spoken-word poetry together in Dublin, where a journalist named Melissa spots their potential. Drawn into Melissa\'s orbit, Frances is reluctantly impressed by the older woman\'s sophisticated home and tall, handsome husband. Private property, Frances believes, is a cultural evil--and Nick, a bored actor who never quite lived up to his potential, looks like patriarchy made flesh. But however amusing their flirtation seems at first, it gives way to a strange intimacy neither of them expect. As Frances tries to keep her life in check, her relationships increasingly resist her control: with Nick, with her difficult and unhappy father, and finally even with Bobbi. Desperate to reconcile herself to the desires and vulnerabilities of her body, Frances\'s intellectual certainties begin to yield to something new: a painful and disorienting way of living from moment But what Afra has seen is so terrible she has gone blind, and so they must embark on a perilous journey through Turkey and Greece towards an uncertain future in Britain. On the way, Nuri is sustained by the knowledge that waiting for them is Mustafa, his cousin and business partner, who has started an apiary and is teaching fellow refugees in Yorkshire to keep bees.\n\nAs Nuri and Afra travel through a broken world, they must confront not only the pain of their own unspeakable loss, but dangers that would overwhelm the bravest of souls.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1448.Karen_Joy_Fowler">About Karen Joy Fowler</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1448.Karen_Joy_Fowler"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1194620963p3/1448.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1448.Karen_Joy_Fowler">Karen Joy Fowler
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7792239638403470069">I was born in Bloomington, Indiana. I was due on Valentine's Day but arrived a week early; my mother blamed this on a really exciting IU basketball game. My father was a psychologist at the University, but not that kind of psychologist. He studied animal behavior, and especially learning. He ran rats through mazes. My mother was a polio survivor, a schoolteacher, and a pioneer in the co-operative </span>
  <span id="freeText7792239638403470069" style="display:none">I was born in Bloomington, Indiana. I was due on Valentine's Day but arrived a week early; my mother blamed this on a really exciting IU basketball game. My father was a psychologist at the University, but not that kind of psychologist. He studied animal behavior, and especially learning. He ran rats through mazes. My mother was a polio survivor, a schoolteacher, and a pioneer in the co-operative nursery school movement. Along with basketball, my family loved books. The day I got my first library card there was a special dinner to celebrate. And before I could read myself, I remember my father reading The Iliad to me, although really he was reading it to my older brother, I just got to be there. A shocking book! And I remember Mary Poppins and Winnie the Pooh in my father's voice and a bunch of other things that weren't movies yet. My parents strongly disapproved of the Disney version of things. Pooh believed in a spoonful of honey, but Mary Poppins did not.<br /><br />I have great memories of Bloomington. Our block was packed with kids and we played enormous games that covered whole blocks of territory, with ten kids to a side. One of my childhood friends was Theodore Deppe, who's now an outstanding poet. I planned to grow up to be a dog trainer myself.<br /><br />Both my parents were raised in southern California and so regarded our time in Indiana as an exile. When I was 11 years old my father was offered a job with Encyclopedia Britannica that necessitated our moving to Palo Alto, California. My parents were thrilled to be coming back. My older brother, for reasons that escape me, was equally pleased. I was devastated.<br /><br />Palo Alto was much more sophisticated than Bloomington. At recess in Bloomington we played baseball, skipped rope, played jacks or marbles depending on the season. In Palo Alto girls my age were already setting their hair, listening to the radio, talking about boys. I considered it a sad trade. The best thing about the sixth-grade was that my teacher, Miss Sarzin, read The Hobbit to us.<br /><br />After reading many more books, I graduated from Palo Alto High in 1968 and went to Berkeley. I was a political science major and an antiwar activist. I was in Berkeley during People's Park, when the city was occupied and there were tanks on the street corners, and I was there during the Jackson State/Kent State killings. I met my husband there. He'd been part of the free speech movement; that was my idea of glamor. We got married the year I graduated and we came to graduate school at UC Davis together.<br /><br />As an undergraduate I had a special interest in India and Gandhi, and a general interest in imperialism. I find the intersection of cultures fascinating, the misunderstandings that occur, the mistakes that are innocently made. I'm not so fascinated by the mistakes that aren't innocent, although there are a good many more of the latter kind. As a graduate student I focused on China and Japan. It's not clear to me what my career goals were — whatever, I had my first child during spring break of the last year of my masters. Six days less than two years later I had a second child. My husband and I still live in Davis, although the kids have left for college and beyond.<br /><br />I decided to try to be a writer on my 30th birthday.<br />All questions answered</span>
  <a data-text-id="7792239638403470069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
